### [1] [Beyond Parallel Sampling: Diverse Query Initialization for Agentic Search](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17209)
*Sidhaarth Murali,João Coelho,Jingjie Ning,João Magalhães,Bruno Martins,Chenyan Xiong*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: DivInit improves parallel agentic search by selecting diverse initial queries from candidate pool to reduce redundancy and increase efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard parallel sampling for agentic search shows diminishing returns due to query redundancy at the first turn - when models issue similar first queries across rollouts, they retrieve overlapping evidence, limiting the effectiveness of breadth scaling.

Method: DivInit is a training-free intervention that draws n candidate queries from a single call, selects k < n diverse seeds, and runs them as parallel trajectories, reducing redundancy and increasing evidence diversity.

Result: Across five open-weight models and eight benchmarks, DivInit consistently improves over standard parallel sampling with average gains of five to seven points on multi-hop QA at matched compute.

Conclusion: Diverse query initialization (DivInit) effectively addresses the limitations of parallel sampling in agentic search by reducing query redundancy and improving the efficiency of breadth scaling.

Abstract: Test-time scaling for agentic search typically increases depth (i.e., more turns and tokens per trajectory) or breadth (i.e., more parallel rollouts). Here we focus on breadth scaling, showing that standard parallel sampling yields diminishing returns, tracing this to query redundancy at the first turn. When models issue similar first queries across rollouts, the threads retrieve overlapping evidence, and subsequent turns are conditioned on this shared retrieval. We address this limitation with DivInit, a training-free intervention at the first turn. Rather than sampling k independent first queries, DivInit draws n candidates from a single call, picks k < n diverse seeds, and runs them as parallel trajectories. Across five open-weight models and eight benchmarks, DivInit consistently improves over standard parallel sampling, with average gains of five to seven points on multi-hop QA at matched compute. Code available at https://github.com/cxcscmu/diverse-query-initialization

</details>


### [2] [When Rules Learn: A Self-Evolving Agent for Legal Case Retrieval](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17220)
*Mingxu Tao,Jiawei Hu,Xian Zhou,Wenpeng Hu,Jiajun Cheng,Yunbo Cao,Zhunchen Luo,Guotong Geng*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes a self-evolving framework for rule-driven query rewriting that enhances BM25 for legal case retrieval without training parameters, using LLM-based agents to iteratively create and refine rewriting rules through automatic evaluation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Despite progress in dense retrieval models, BM25 remains a strong baseline for legal case retrieval due to legal language complexity and need for precise lexical alignment. This motivates development of methods to enhance BM25 without parameter training.

Method: A self-evolving framework with an LLM-based agent that: 1) creates rewriting rules, 2) plans validation experiments over rule combinations, 3) eliminates ineffective rules based on historical feedback. Operates in an automatic evaluation environment for iterative refinement.

Result: Outperforms non-evolutionary baselines (human-designed rules and greedy rule selection) on Chinese legal case retrieval benchmark LeCaRD-v2, especially with high-capacity core LLM. Analysis shows LLM's ability to leverage previous results and intrinsic knowledge of rule elimination are critical.

Conclusion: The proposed self-evolving framework effectively enhances BM25 for legal case retrieval without training, demonstrating LLM agents can successfully refine rule sets through iterative learning and feedback incorporation.

Abstract: Legal case retrieval remains challenging due to the complexity of legal language and the need for precise lexical alignment between queries and relevant cases. Although dense retrieval models have achieved notable progress, empirical studies show that BM25 continues to serve as a strong baseline in this domain. It motivates us to propose a self-evolving framework for rule-driven query rewriting that enhances BM25 without any parameter training. The framework equips an LLM-based agent with an automatic evaluation environment, enabling it to iteratively create rewriting rules, plan validation experiments over rule combinations, and eliminate ineffective rules based on historical feedbacks. We evaluate our method on the Chinese legal case retrieval benchmark LeCaRD-v2. Experimental results demonstrate that the proposed framework outperforms non-evolutionary baselines, including human-designed rules and greedy rule selection, particularly when powered by a highcapacity core LLM. We also conduct detailed analyses to investigate the mechanisms underlying self-evolution. Our findings reveal that LLM's capabilities to leverage previous experimental results and its intrinsic knowledge of rule elimination play critical roles in refining the rule set via self-evolution.

</details>


### [3] [SkillChain-Gym: A Benchmark for Reskilling-Aware Production-Inventory Control under Disruptions](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17266)
*Carlos Eduardo Sanoja*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SkillChain-Gym is a benchmark for production planning with reskilling constraints, evaluating policies that balance worker training against production capacity under skills certification, forgetting, and disruptions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional operations benchmarks treat labor as exogenous, but modern production requires treating workforce capability as a decision variable due to skills maintenance, certification lapses, and competition between training and production time.

Method: Developed SkillChain-Gym benchmark with single-site environment featuring worker skill-state dynamics, hard threshold certification, forgetting effects, and training actions constrained by same time budget as production. Includes disruption scenarios, feasibility modes, deterministic replay, and comprehensive metrics.

Result: Training-capable policies outperform production-only baseline. Maintenance training is necessary under forgetting even without disruptions. Adaptive training helps when bottlenecks are visible, while static cross-training plans act as insurance under surprise shocks. No policy dominates across all regimes.

Conclusion: Production planning must integrate skill maintenance as a core decision variable. Different policies suit different regimes depending on visibility of bottlenecks, capacity slack, and forgetting rates, motivating forecast-driven controllers that dynamically choose between skill insurance and reactive training.

Abstract: Production planning increasingly has to treat workforce capability as a decision variable: certifications lapse when skills are not maintained, new products require skills the current workforce does not hold, and reskilling competes for the same worker hours needed for production. Existing operations benchmarks usually treat labor as exogenous, while workforce-planning models with skills and learning are rarely released as reusable testbeds. We introduce SkillChain-Gym, a benchmark specification for reskilling-aware production-inventory control: a single-site environment with stylized worker skill-state dynamics, hard threshold certification, forgetting, and capacity-consuming training actions constrained by the same per-worker time budget as production. The benchmark includes seed-controlled disruption scenarios, three feasibility modes with projection diagnostics, deterministic replay, and metrics covering operations, resilience, capability growth, and training-access distribution. We evaluate production-only, reactive adaptive, water-filling adaptive, and static-insurance policies with budget variants over 60-shift horizons with paired statistical tests. The results are regime-dependent rather than a ranking. Training-capable policies dominate the production-only baseline, and maintenance training is necessary under forgetting even without disruptions. Among training-capable classes, adaptive training helps when bottlenecks are visible in the forecast, while a lean static cross-training plan, a deliberately favorable comparator whose structure encodes relevant skill contingencies, acts as strong insurance under surprise shocks and absenteeism. Capacity slack and the forgetting rate govern the boundary between these regimes. No policy class dominates across regimes, motivating forecast-driven controllers that decide when to buy skill insurance and when to react.

</details>


### [4] [LegalHalluLens: Typed Hallucination Auditing and Calibrated Multi-Agent Debate for Trustworthy Legal AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.18021)
*Lalit Yadav,Akshaj Gurugubelli*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LegalHalluLens is an auditing framework that identifies specific types of legal hallucinations in AI systems and measures risk direction, revealing hidden failure patterns that aggregate metrics conceal.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current AI hallucination metrics for legal workflows (~52% average) fail to show where errors concentrate and in which direction they run (omissions vs. inventions), leaving compliance officers without actionable signals for trustworthy deployment.

Method: Three-component framework: 1) Typed hallucination profiles across four legal claim categories (numeric, temporal, obligation/entitlement, factual); 2) Risk Direction Index (RDI) reducing omission-invention bias to a single scalar; 3) Typed debate pipeline calibrated to both magnitudes and directions using asymmetric gates and targeted challenges.

Result: Revealed 38-40 pp gap between obligation/numeric and temporal claims that aggregate reporting hides; showed systems with same 52% rate can have opposite RDIs; debate pipeline reduced fabricated detections by 45%; matched commercial APIs with much smaller backbone (4B vs larger parameters).

Conclusion: Typed profiles and RDI surface hidden failure modes and serve as calibration inputs for debate pipelines, enabling direction-aware procurement, accountability, and agent design for legal AI deployed in practice.

Abstract: AI systems deployed in legal workflows hallucinate at rates that aggregate metrics report at ~52%, but this average conceals where errors concentrate and in which direction they run, leaving compliance officers without an actionable signal for trustworthy deployment. We present LegalHalluLens, an auditing framework with three components: typed hallucination profiles across four legally-motivated claim categories (numeric, temporal, obligation/entitlement, factual) over CUAD (Hendrycks et al., 2021); a Risk Direction Index (RDI) that reduces omission-versus-invention bias to a single deployment-comparable scalar; and a typed debate pipeline calibrated to both magnitudes and directions. Across 510 contracts and 249,252 clause-level instances we measure a within-model gap of approximately 38-40 pp between obligation/numeric and temporal claims that aggregate reporting hides, and show that two systems with matched 52% rates can carry opposite RDIs. The debate pipeline reduces fabricated detections by 45% with per-category gains tracking the diagnosis, matching commercial APIs with a substantially smaller backbone (4B active parameters). Typed profiles and RDI surface failure modes that aggregate metrics hide; we further show these diagnostics serve as calibration inputs for multi-agent debate pipelines, where Skeptic challenges and asymmetric gates targeted at measured failure modes outperform generically-tuned debate. The framework supports direction-aware procurement, accountability, and agent design for legal AI deployed in the wild.

</details>


### [5] [Skill-Constrained Model Predictive Control for Resilient Manufacturing Supply Chains](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17269)
*Carlos Eduardo Sanoja*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A study of predictive control in skill-constrained production-inventory systems where workforce training competes with production for worker hours, finding predictive control helps only when skill/labor bottlenecks are forecastable early enough for training completion.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: In production-inventory systems, production requires certified workers, certifications decay without maintenance, and training competes with production for the same limited worker hours. This creates a dynamic trade-off between using worker time for immediate production versus building/maintaining future certified capacity.

Method: Developed a closed-loop skill-constrained model predictive controller (MPC) that solves finite-horizon mixed-integer programs over production, inventory, backlog, and training decisions at every shift. Used binary predicted certification variables with hard production eligibility constraints and an interpretable terminal value function. Evaluated on synthetic SkillChain-Gym scenarios including various shocks, absenteeism, forecast modes, and capacity sweeps against multiple baselines.

Result: No single policy class dominates - results show regime dependence. Predictive control helps when skill/labor bottlenecks are forecastable early enough for training to complete. Lean static insurance plans remain competitive under surprise shocks, near capacity boundaries, and where pre-shock slack makes insurance cheap. Forecastability, not just adaptivity, determines when predictive control is valuable.

Conclusion: The value of predictive control in skill-constrained systems depends critically on forecastability of skill/labor bottlenecks. There's no universally superior approach - the choice between predictive control, static insurance, or reactive approaches should be based on the specific operational regime and forecast quality available.

Abstract: In skill-constrained production-inventory systems, the qualified human capacity available tomorrow depends on training decisions made today: production requires certified workers, certifications decay unless maintained, and training consumes the same scarce worker hours that production needs now. We study a closed-loop skill-constrained model predictive controller that, at every shift, solves a finite-horizon mixed-integer program over production, inventory, backlog, and training, with binary predicted certification, hard production eligibility, and an interpretable terminal value that prices certified-capacity gaps at the horizon boundary; only the first-period action is applied before replanning. On synthetic, seed-controlled SkillChain-Gym scenarios - announced and surprise new-skill shocks, demand shocks, absenteeism, forecast- and availability-quality modes, capacity-boundary and training-rate sweeps, and negative controls - we evaluate the controller against production-only and maintenance-only ablations, static cross-training insurance plans, and a strong reactive heuristic, under an ex-ante locked configuration and paired statistics. The result is regime dependence, not superiority: no policy class dominates. Predictive control helps when skill or labor bottlenecks are forecastable early enough for training to complete; lean static insurance remains hard to beat under surprise shocks, near the demand-capacity boundary, and wherever pre-shock slack makes insurance cheap. Attribution ablations separate certification maintenance, re-acquisition of lapsed certifications, and greenfield skill acquisition. Forecastability, not adaptivity per se, decides when predictive control pays.

</details>


### [6] [ProvenanceGuard: Source-Aware Factuality Verification for MCP-Based LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.18037)
*Ander Alvarez,Santhiya Rajan,Samuel Mugel,Román Orús*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: ProvenanceGuard is a source-aware verification system for LLM agents using MCP that detects when answers cite wrong sources, called "cross-source conflation," and can repair incorrect attributions.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard factuality metrics miss provenance-sensitive failures where claims are supported by evidence but attributed to wrong sources (cross-source conflation), which is critical for reliable MCP-based agents using heterogeneous evidence sources.

Method: Consumes MCP traces with tool/source IDs, decomposes answers into atomic claims, routes claims to source-specific evidence, checks support with NLI and token-alignment proxy, compares stated attribution with routed source, and returns per-claim verdicts with answer-level allow/block decisions.

Result: Achieves block F1 0.802 and source accuracy 0.858 on 40-trace held-out set, outperforms source-blind baselines. On harder multi-source benchmark, block F1 reaches 0.846 but source-plus-relation accuracy drops to 0.229. Repair-and-reverify resolves all blocked answers, and system detects all 50 injected attribution swaps.

Conclusion: Source attribution is an independent axis for factuality verification in MCP-based agents, and ProvenanceGuard effectively detects cross-source conflation while enabling correction through repair mechanisms.

Abstract: Tool-using LLM agents increasingly use the Model Context Protocol (MCP) to answer from heterogeneous evidence sources, including search, APIs, databases, clinical records, and formulary tools. Standard factuality metrics usually test whether an answer is supported by pooled evidence, missing a provenance-sensitive failure mode: a claim may be supported somewhere while being attributed to the wrong source. We call this cross-source conflation.
  We introduce ProvenanceGuard, a source-aware verifier for MCP-grounded answers. It consumes captured MCP traces with stable tool IDs, source IDs, and raw outputs; decomposes answers into atomic claims; routes claims to source-specific evidence; checks support with NLI and a token-alignment proxy; compares stated attribution with the routed source; and returns per-claim verdicts plus an answer-level allow/block decision. Blocked answers can be repaired with retrieval-augmented answer revision and re-verified.
  We evaluate on 281 medical-domain MCP-agent traces. A 266-trace adjudicated subset yields 2,325 LLM-assisted claim labels split by trace; 361 held-out labels are human-verified. On the 40-trace held-out split, ProvenanceGuard achieves block F1 0.802 and source accuracy 0.858 over 260 source-eligible claims, outperforming source-blind baselines that do not emit claim-to-source IDs. On a harder multi-source benchmark it reaches block F1 0.846, while source-plus-relation accuracy drops to 0.229, showing that exact source ownership remains difficult with semantically close sources. Repair-and-reverify resolves all blocked answers in the full trace set, often via conservative fallback. In 50 controlled clinical conflation probes, ProvenanceGuard detects all injected attribution swaps with no retained wrong attribution. These results show that source attribution is an independent axis for factuality verification in MCP-based agents.

</details>


### [7] [Nothing from Something: Can a Language Model Discover 0?](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17289)
*Phoebe Zeng,Thomas L. Griffiths,Brenden M. Lake*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Language models can't independently discover the concept of "zero" at test time, but can learn it with training data, and language pretraining reduces the training examples needed by ~50%.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how AI systems can push beyond training data and achieve genuine mathematical discovery, using "zero" discovery as a case study for examining if modern AI models can expand mathematical horizons independently.

Method: Using simple arithmetic as a case study, evaluating whether GPT-2 size language models can independently discover the concept of "zero". Testing models both with and without language pretraining, and measuring improvements after training on tens to hundreds of zero examples.

Result: 1) GPT-2 size language models cannot independently discover zero at test time regardless of language pretraining. 2) Models improve substantially after training on tens/hundreds of zero examples. 3) Language pretraining reduces required training examples by approximately 50%.

Conclusion: Language abilities can scaffold mathematical discovery in neural models, as shown by reduced training requirements for zero concept learning, but models still need explicit training rather than pure independent discovery.

Abstract: AI systems based on artificial neural networks are being developed with aspirations of pushing the boundary of human mathematical knowledge. A key question for these systems is how much they can reach beyond their training data. Mathematical discovery requires a strong form of out of distribution generalization; the ability to hypothesize genuinely new - and potentially logically more powerful - mathematical structures. It has been hypothesized that language abilities support such generalizations in human cognition. In this work, we use simple arithmetic as a case study for examining how modern AI models could expand their mathematical horizons, evaluating whether these models can independently discover the concept of "zero". We show that We show that (1) language models of a GPT-2 size are unable to perform this generalization at test time regardless of language pretraining, but (2) models can improve substantially after training on tens or hundreds of examples of zero. Additionally, we find that language pretraining reduces the number of required examples by approximately $50\%$, showing that language abilities can scaffold mathematical discovery in neural models.

</details>


### [8] [DRFLOW: A Deep Research Benchmark for Personalized Workflow Prediction](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.18191)
*Md Tawkat Islam Khondaker,Raymond Li,Muhammad Abdul-Mageed,Laks V. S. Lakshmanan,Issam H. Laradji*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: DRFLOW is a new benchmark for evaluating AI agents' ability to predict personalized workflows (action-step sequences) from heterogeneous sources, addressing enterprise tasks beyond simple report generation.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current deep research systems focus mainly on generating reports and summaries, but many enterprise tasks require identifying concrete workflows - sequences of action steps needed to accomplish specific tasks. There's a need for systems that can determine workflows for questions like "How do I request new headcount given a fixed budget?"

Method: The authors introduce DRFLOW benchmark with 100 tasks across five domains, requiring agents to identify relevant evidence from scattered sources (3,900+ sources) and predict correct action-step sequences (1,246 reference workflow steps). They define seven diagnostic metrics covering factual grounding, step recovery, structural ordering, condition resolution, and personalization.

Result: They present DRFLOW-Agent (DRFA), a workflow-oriented reference agent that improves over strong baseline agents by up to 10.02% average F1 score. However, substantial room for improvement remains across workflow metrics, indicating that predicting complete and correct personalized workflows is still challenging.

Conclusion: Predicting personalized workflows from heterogeneous sources is a challenging frontier for deep research systems, and the DRFLOW benchmark provides a framework for evaluating progress in this important enterprise capability.

Abstract: Deep research (DR) systems are increasingly used for complex information-seeking tasks, but existing works mainly focus on generating reports and summaries. In contrast, many enterprise tasks instead require an agent to identify concrete workflows which is a sequence of action-steps. For example, rather than summarizing budgeting policies, an agent should be able to determine the steps needed to answer a question such as: "How do I request new headcount given a fixed budget?". Therefore, we introduce DRFLOW, a benchmark for evaluating personalized workflows predicted by agents from heterogeneous sources. Each task requires the agent to identify relevant evidence from scattered sources, then use that evidence to predict the correct action-step sequence for the user's task. DRFLOW contains 100 tasks across five domains, with 1,246 reference workflow steps grounded in more than 3,900 sources. We define seven diagnostic metrics covering factual grounding, step recovery, structural ordering, condition resolution, and personalization. We further present DRFLOW-Agent (DRFA), a workflow-oriented reference agent to predict personalized workflow. We show that although DRFA improves over strong baseline agents (upto 10.02% average F1 score), there is substantial room for improvement remains across these workflow metrics, indicating that predicting complete and correct personalized workflows remains a challenging frontier for deep research.

</details>


### [9] [Quantifying Consistency in LLM Logical Reasoning via Structural Uncertainty](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17312)
*Baishali Chaudhury,Mengdie Flora Wang,Hyunji Hayley Park,Rahul Ghosh,Sungmin Hong,Jae Oh Woo*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes structural uncertainty, a consistency-aware framework for evaluating LLM reliability through self-preference rankings over multiple reasoning paths.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing methods assess LLM reliability through output dispersion (sampled answer differences), but this discards complementary signals about whether models can consistently rank competing reasoning candidates. LLMs often produce unstable, contradictory, or inconsistently ranked reasoning paths in multi-step deductive reasoning.

Method: Generate multiple candidate solutions for a query, have the model judge pairwise preferences among its own outputs, aggregate self-preferences into ranking distributions using Bradley-Terry modeling with PageRank, and decompose the signal into two entropy-based components: across-trial ranking instability and within-trial candidate ambiguity.

Result: Structural signals complement answer dispersion: on logical/mathematical reasoning tasks, the combination improves unreliable instance identification. On factual retrieval, structural signal collapses to uniformity, diagnosing regime boundaries. Within-trial ambiguity correlates positively with correctness, while across-trial instability correlates negatively with reliability.

Conclusion: Structural uncertainty is not a universal confidence estimator but a regime-sensitive evaluator of logical reasoning consistency, providing complementary reliability signals to existing dispersion-based methods.

Abstract: Large language models can arrive at the same answer through reasoning paths that are unstable, contradictory, or difficult to rank consistently -- a failure mode especially prevalent in multi-step deductive reasoning. Existing methods assess reliability primarily through output dispersion -- measuring how much sampled answers differ -- but this discards a complementary signal: whether the model can consistently rank competing reasoning candidates. We propose structural uncertainty, a consistency-aware framework derived from the stability of self-preference-induced rankings over sampled reasoning solutions. Given a query, we generate multiple candidate solutions and ask the model to judge pairwise preferences among its own outputs. We aggregate self-preferences into ranking distributions via Bradley-Terry modeling with PageRank, and decompose the signal into two entropy-based components: across-trial ranking instability and within-trial candidate ambiguity. Across five LLMs and eight benchmarks, structural signals provide information complementary to answer dispersion: on logical and mathematical reasoning tasks, the combination improves identification of unreliable instances, while on factual retrieval the structural signal collapses toward uniformity, diagnosing a regime boundary where reasoning-level consistency evaluation is uninformative. The two components relate differently to accuracy: within-trial ambiguity correlates positively with correctness -- consistent with settings where multiple plausible solution paths remain competitive -- while across-trial instability correlates negatively, signaling unreliable reasoning. Structural uncertainty is best understood not as a universal confidence estimator, but as a regime-sensitive evaluator of logical reasoning consistency.

</details>


### [10] [MemTrace: Probing What Final Accuracy Misses in Long-Term Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17328)
*Xianxuan Long,Zhikai Chen,Shenglai Zeng,Shouren Wang,Kai Guo,Jiliang Tang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: MemTrace is a new benchmark that evaluates LLM agent long-term memory by testing individual knowledge points (user facts) across three dimensions: memory age, question type, and evidence conditions, revealing that the main bottleneck is evidence utilization rather than retrieval.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current evaluation methods for LLM agent memory score question rows independently, which prevents understanding how individual facts behave under changing conditions. The paper introduces MemTrace to address this limitation and provide more fine-grained insights into memory system performance.

Method: The authors developed MemTrace benchmark with knowledge points as the unit of measurement. Each fact is probed along three dimensions: memory age (sessions since appearance), question type (current state, earlier state, trajectory of change), and evidence condition (present, missing, contradicted-by-false-premise). They evaluated 13 memory-system configurations across four paradigms.

Result: Similar pooled accuracy scores hide different failure patterns: systems that recover current and earlier states don't necessarily track changes, and safe abstention doesn't imply correcting false premises. The dominant bottleneck is evidence use rather than retrieval - when systems fail, evidence was retrievable 10 times more often than missing.

Conclusion: Improving long-term memory in LLM agents requires better utilization of reachable evidence, not just more storage or enhanced retrieval capabilities. The MemTrace benchmark provides a more nuanced way to evaluate memory systems by focusing on knowledge point behavior across multiple dimensions.

Abstract: LLM agents increasingly maintain long-term memory of user facts across sessions. Yet such memory is usually evaluated by aggregating accuracy over question rows or episodes. Because this approach scores question rows independently, even when several questions probe the same fact, it cannot show how that fact behaves as conditions change. We introduce MemTrace, a benchmark whose unit of measurement is the knowledge point: a single typed fact about the user, rather than an individual question. MemTrace probes each fact along three controlled dimensions: memory age, defined by how many sessions ago the fact appeared in the history; question type, covering current state, earlier state, and trajectory of change; and evidence condition, covering present, missing, and contradicted-by-false-premise settings. Evaluating 13 memory-system configurations across four paradigms, we find that similar pooled accuracy hides different failures: recovering a fact's current and earlier states does not imply tracking how it changed, and safe abstention does not imply correcting a false premise. The dominant bottleneck is evidence use, not retrieval: when systems fail, the evidence was retrievable 10 times more often than it was missing. These results suggest that improving long-term memory requires better use of reachable evidence, not simply more storage or retrieval.

</details>


### [11] [SpeechDx: A Multi-Task Benchmark for Clinical Speech AI](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17339)
*Sejal Bhalla,Larry Kieu,Aina Merchant,Eyal de Lara,Alex Mariakakis*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SpeechDx is a large-scale benchmark for clinical speech AI that evaluates 27 tasks across 12 datasets, structured by speech production stages (conceptualization, formulation, articulation), with systematic evaluation of 12 state-of-the-art audio encoders including zero-shot cross-condition transfer.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current clinical speech AI methods progress through isolated condition-specific studies, making results difficult to compare and generalization difficult to assess. There's a need for a unified framework to enable systematic evaluation and comparison of speech AI models across diverse health conditions.

Method: Introduced SpeechDx benchmark with 12 datasets and 27 tasks across diverse health conditions, structured by three stages of speech production disruption. Systematically evaluated 12 state-of-the-art audio encoders across all tasks, including zero-shot cross-condition transfer experiments with limited labeled data settings.

Result: Large-scale speech models represent the strongest overall baselines, domain-specific models improve performance only on closely matched tasks, and no current representation generalizes reliably across the clinical speech landscape.

Conclusion: SpeechDx establishes a shared evaluation framework for tracking progress toward general-purpose clinical speech representations, addressing the fragmentation in clinical speech AI research and enabling better comparison of model generalization capabilities.

Abstract: Speech offers a uniquely informative window into health by simultaneously engaging neurological, motor, respiratory, and vocal systems. Current clinical speech AI methods have largely progressed through isolated condition-specific studies, making results difficult to compare and generalization difficult to assess. We introduce SpeechDx, a large-scale benchmark for clinical speech AI spanning 12 datasets and 27 tasks across diverse health conditions. To enable evaluation across shared clinical mechanisms, SpeechDx structures tasks by the stage of speech production they disrupt: conceptualization, formulation, and articulation. The benchmark tests generalization by including tasks with limited labeled data and evaluating the same health condition across multiple datasets, distinguishing clinically meaningful patterns from dataset artefacts. We systematically evaluate 12 state-of-the-art audio encoders across all tasks and under zero-shot cross-condition transfer. Results show that large-scale speech models represent the strongest overall baselines, domain-specific models improve performance only on closely matched tasks, and no current representation generalizes reliably across the clinical speech landscape. SpeechDx establishes a shared evaluation framework for tracking progress toward general-purpose clinical speech representations

</details>


### [12] [Distributed General-Purpose Agent Networks: Architecture, Key Mechanisms, and Prototypes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17368)
*Shengli Zhang,Deen Ma,Zibin Lin,Taotao Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A framework for distributed general-purpose agent networks where heterogeneous AI agents can discover each other, establish trust, and collaborate on open-ended tasks through semantic protocols and reputation mechanisms.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Single AI agents are constrained by local resources, tools, and governance boundaries. There's a need for open peer-to-peer networks where heterogeneous agents from different devices/environments can collaborate on complex tasks by discovering each other, establishing trust, and executing multi-step workflows.

Method: Proposes a layered architecture with protocol adaptation layer connecting task semantics to network operations. Key mechanisms include: 1) semantic announcement propagation using bodyless gossip with sequential logs for collaborator discovery; 2) BAID-based identity binding with MG-EigenTrust reputation for cooperation governance; 3) semantic-gradient mechanism design with Stackelberg-style mechanism-generation loop driven by semantic attribution feedback for open task execution.

Result: Prototype results show overhead for BAID-style tiered verification and mechanism-level simulations of MG-EigenTrust under cross-topic disguise-collusion attacks. The framework provides a foundation for open, trustworthy, scalable agent collaboration.

Conclusion: The proposed distributed agent network architecture addresses fundamental challenges in agent collaboration through semantic protocols, verifiable identity/reputation systems, and mechanism design, enabling open and scalable cooperation between heterogeneous AI agents.

Abstract: Large language models have accelerated the transition from passive conversational assistants to autonomous agents that can understand goals, plan actions, invoke tools, and execute multi-step tasks. Yet the capability of a single agent remains constrained by its local data, tool permissions, runtime environment, and governance boundary. This paper studies distributed general-purpose agent networks: open peer-to-peer networks in which heterogeneous agents deployed on personal devices, edge nodes, or autonomous computing environments can discover one another, establish trust, negotiate cooperation rules, and execute open-ended tasks. We argue that such networks cannot be obtained by simply combining existing peer-to-peer overlays with conventional multi-agent systems. Unlike traditional P2P networks, agent networks must propagate semantic declarations about intentions, capabilities, states, and cooperation constraints. We therefore propose a layered architecture centered on a protocol adaptation layer that connects upper-level task semantics with lower-level network operations. Based on this architecture, the paper identifies three core mechanism problems: semantic announcement propagation for collaborator discovery, verifiable identity and multi-topic reputation for cooperation governance, and semantic-gradient mechanism design for open task execution. For each problem, we present a technical route, including bodyless gossip with sequential logs, BAID-based identity binding with MG-EigenTrust reputation, and a Stackelberg-style mechanism-generation loop driven by semantic attribution feedback. We further report prototype overhead results for BAID-style tiered verification and mechanism-level simulations of MG-EigenTrust under cross-topic disguise-collusion attacks. The resulting framework provides a system-level foundation for open, trustworthy, and scalable agent collaboration.

</details>


### [13] [Treatment Response Optimized Clinical Decision Support AI System via Digital Twin Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17405)
*Xinyu Qin,Anil K. Sood,Ruiheng Yu,Sara Corvigno,Elaine Stur,Lu Wang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: An online adaptive AI framework for clinical decision support that combines treatment effect estimation, patient digital twins, and reinforcement learning for real-time personalized treatment recommendations with safety constraints and clinician oversight.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clinical decision support AI systems need to adapt to evolving patient conditions in real-time while maintaining strict safety constraints, requiring a system that can continuously learn, simulate treatments, and operate safely with clinician supervision.

Method: Integrates three components: 1) Treatment Effect estimation to quantify clinical benefits, 2) Patient Digital Twin to simulate treatment trajectories, and 3) Reinforcement Learning for sequential decision-making. Includes safety mechanisms: rule-based monitoring of vital signs and clinician review for cases with internal model disagreement. The system learns from historical medical records and operates in a continuous learning loop.

Result: Validated on both synthetic clinical simulator and real-world ovarian cancer dataset from TCGA. The method demonstrated superior effectiveness and stability in treatment recommendations compared to standard computational baselines, with low latency and requiring expert consultation for only a minority of cases.

Conclusion: The framework represents a safe, clinician-supervised tool for personalized medicine that can continuously improve through practical use, showing promise for real-world clinical deployment with appropriate safety safeguards.

Abstract: Clinical decision support AI systems (CDSASs) must adapt to evolving patient conditions in real-time while adhering to strict safety constraints. We present an online adaptive framework that integrates Treatment Effect (TE) estimation to quantify clinical benefits, a patient Digital Twin (DT) to simulate treatment trajectories, and Reinforcement Learning (RL) for sequential decision-making. The AI system is initially trained on historical medical records and operates in a continuous learning loop. To ensure safety, a rule-based module monitors vital signs and blocks contraindicated treatments. Cases with strong internal model disagreement are flagged for clinician review, simulated in our experiments via a pre-trained outcome model. We validate our framework using both a synthetic clinical simulator and a real-world ovarian cancer dataset from The Cancer Genome Atlas (TCGA). In both simulated and clinical settings, our method demonstrated superior effectiveness and stability in recommending treatments compared to standard computational baselines. Furthermore, the AI system maintains low latency and requires expert consultation for only a minority of cases in our experimental validation, demonstrating its potential as a safe, clinician-supervised tool for personalized medicine that continuously improves through practical use.

### [14] [Incumbent Advantage: Brand Bias and Cognitive Manipulation Dynamics in LLM Recommendation Systems](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17443)
*Xi Chu,Yupeng Hou*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: LLMs exhibit conditional brand monopolies in product recommendations, but these can be broken by small rating advantages or fabricated marketing claims, creating a social dilemma in generative engine optimization strategies.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: As LLMs become a major channel for product discovery, there is a need to understand how brands compete in this new environment, particularly for experience goods like skincare where consumers rely on brand reputation.

Method: Three experiments across three commercial LLMs (GPT-4o-mini, Claude Sonnet, Gemini 3 Flash) examining skincare recommendations, with robustness checks on search goods. Experiments test conditional brand dominance, authority-style marketing language impact, and multi-brand GEO competition.

Result: (1) Conditional Monopoly: well-known brands get 100% recommendations when specs are equal, but dominance disappears with less than +0.1-star rating advantage; (2) Fabricated clinical-evidence claims break monopoly at Bias Surplus Value = +0.17 rating points; (3) Social dilemma in GEO: when all brands optimize, individual payoff drops drastically and non-participating brands get zero recommendations.

Conclusion: Generative engine optimization should be studied both as a security risk and as an emerging marketing practice that shapes market competition, with important implications for brand strategy and consumer welfare.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are becoming a major way for consumers to find products, but we do not yet understand how brands compete in this new channel. We study brand dynamics in LLM recommendations using skincare products -- a category where consumers cannot easily judge quality before buying and must rely on brand reputation -- across three commercial LLMs (GPT-4o-mini, Claude Sonnet, Gemini 3 Flash), with a robustness check on search goods. In three experiments, we find: (1) a Conditional Monopoly where well-known brands get recommended 100% of the time (IAI = 10.0) when all products have the same specifications, but this dominance disappears with less than a +0.1-star rating advantage for a competitor; (2) authority-style marketing language, including fabricated clinical-evidence claims, breaks this monopoly at a Bias Surplus Value equal to +0.17 rating points, with each model responding differently; and (3) a social dilemma in multi-brand GEO competition: when all brands adopt the same optimization strategy, individual payoff falls from +0.802 to +0.007 in our payoff proxy, and non-participating brands receive zero recommendations in our tests. Our results suggest that generative engine optimization (GEO) should be studied not only as a security risk, but also as an emerging marketing practice that shapes market competition.

</details>


### [15] [A Machine-Learned Comorbidity Index](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17450)
*Suleman Baloch,Kishlay Jha,Alberto M. Segre,Philip M. Polgreen,Bijaya Adhikari*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: A machine-learned comorbidity index (MLCI) that uses nHSIC to create unified risk scores across multiple clinical outcomes, outperforming traditional mortality-centric scores like Charlson and Elixhauser.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Traditional comorbidity scores like Charlson and Elixhauser have two key limitations: 1) they are mortality-centric and don't align well with other clinical outcomes, and 2) their linear, rule-based structure cannot capture nonlinear, outcome-specific risk relationships.

Method: Proposes a Machine-Learned Comorbidity Index (MLCI) that maps diagnosis codes to a single scalar by maximizing the normalized Hilbert-Schmidt Independence Criterion (nHSIC) between the learned score and multiple clinical outcomes. This captures nonlinear risk-outcome dependence and is supported by theoretical characterization of when unified admission-level ordering can be achieved across outcomes.

Result: Empirical results on multiple benchmark electronic health record (EHR) datasets show that MLCI outperforms strong baselines across multiple evaluation metrics.

Conclusion: MLCI addresses limitations of traditional comorbidity scores by providing a unified, outcome-informed risk score that captures nonlinear relationships and works across multiple clinical outcomes, demonstrating superior performance in EHR data.

Abstract: Traditional comorbidity scores (e.g., Charlson and Elixhauser) are widely used for risk adjustment and patient stratification, but they have two key limitations: (i) they are largely mortality-centric and do not align well with other clinical outcomes, and (ii) their linear, rule-based structure cannot capture nonlinear, outcome-specific risk relationships. We propose a Machine-Learned Comorbidity Index (MLCI) that maps diagnosis codes to a single scalar by maximizing the normalized Hilbert-Schmidt Independence Criterion (nHSIC) between the learned score and multiple clinical outcomes. MLCI captures nonlinear risk-outcome dependence and is supported by a theory that characterizes when a unified, informative admission-level ordering can be achieved across outcomes. Empirical results on multiple benchmark electronic health record (EHR) datasets show that MLCI outperforms strong baselines across multiple evaluation metrics.

</details>


### [16] [MapSatisfyBench: Benchmarking Satisfaction-Aware Map Agents through Behavior-Grounded Implicit Decision Factors](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17453)
*Lubin Bai,Mengyu Cao,Sixue Wang,Zhongwei Wan,Yue Pan,Jiale Hou,Xiang Li,Xiuyuan Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper introduces MapSatisfyBench, a benchmark for evaluating how well map-service LLM agents can identify and satisfy users' implicit decision factors from underspecified queries, shifting evaluation from task completion to user satisfaction.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Users often express map service needs informally with underspecified queries containing unspoken implicit factors critical for satisfaction. Current evaluation focuses on task completion but fails to assess agents' ability to proactively recover and satisfy these implicit factors.

Method: Proposes a restore-identify-filter framework: reconstructs complete user needs from behavior-chain evidence, identifies implicit decision factors, and retains only those supported by pre-query evidence. Used to construct MapSatisfyBench from large-scale real-world anonymized user data with ground truth annotation from five dimensions.

Result: Current agents perform well on explicit task completion but struggle with satisfying implicit decision factors and proactively acquiring evidence needed for satisfaction-aware decisions. The benchmark enables full-chain evaluation of satisfaction-aware map agents.

Conclusion: MapSatisfyBench establishes a new benchmark for shifting map-agent evaluation from task completion toward satisfaction-aware spatial decision making, addressing the gap in assessing implicit factor recovery and satisfaction in daily interaction scenarios.

Abstract: Large language model agents are increasingly integrated into map services. Since map services are embedded in everyday-life scenarios rather than professional task settings, users often express their needs informally, resulting in underspecified queries with many unspoken needs, namely, implicit decision factors that are critical for user satisfaction. Although clarification is an effective way to mitigate this issue, it increases user burden in daily interaction, and a capable agent should first proactively recover such factors from available information sources. However, evaluating this ability is challenging. The first challenge is to determine which implicit decision factors are suitable for evaluation. A factor is evaluable only if it affects user acceptance and can be recovered from information available to the agent before it responds. Second, user satisfaction cannot be reliably represented by a single reference answer, requiring a benchmark that converts satisfaction-relevant factors into objective and quantifiable evaluation targets. To address these challenges, we propose a restore-identify-filter framework that reconstructs complete user needs from behavior-chain evidence, identifies implicit decision factors, and retains only those supported by pre-query evidence. Building on this methodology, we construct MapSatisfyBench from large-scale, real-world anonymized user data and annotate ground truth from five dimensions and enables full-chain evaluation of satisfaction-aware map agents. Experiments show that current agents generally perform well on explicit task completion, but remain limited in satisfying implicit decision factors and proactively acquiring the evidence needed for satisfaction-aware decisions. These findings establish MapSatisfyBench as a benchmark for shifting map-agent evaluation from task completion toward satisfaction-aware spatial decision making.

### [17] [Dissecting model behavior through agent trajectories](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17454)
*Gaurav Gupta,Vatshank Chaturvedi,Jun Huan,Anoop Deoras*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: The paper identifies the "intent-execution gap" in AI agents where model capabilities don't translate to agent performance due to misalignment between models and their harnesses, and introduces Simple Strands Agent (SSA) as a customizable harness that improves performance and enables detailed analysis of agent behavior.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AI agent performance is fundamentally a systems problem, not just a modeling one. There's a significant gap between model assumptions and harness behavior that prevents models' full capabilities from translating into agent performance. This "intent-execution gap" needs to be minimized for effective agent design.

Method: Developed Simple Strands Agent (SSA), a simple and customizable harness designed to capture common patterns that generalize across different model families (Claude, Gemini, GPT, Grok, Qwen) while accommodating some model-specific preferences. Analyzed 138k agent trajectories in code state-spaces to study problem-solving behavior beyond simple pass@1 metrics.

Result: 1) Reproduced or improved on pass@1 performance across diverse model families on popular agentic benchmarks (SWE-Pro, SWE-Verified, Terminal-Bench-2). 2) Through trajectory analysis, identified model-level differences in problem-solving behavior using finer-grained metrics like edit frequency, testing activity, and phase-transitions, revealing how models allocate effort across different stages of autonomous problem solving.

Conclusion: Harness-model alignment is as important as other design aspects like tools and execution loops for AI agent performance. The intent-execution gap must be minimized to translate model capabilities into agent performance. SSA demonstrates that proper harness design can reveal deeper insights about model behavior beyond simple performance metrics.

Abstract: AI agent performance is not just a modeling problem, it is fundamentally a systems problem. The advanced capabilities of models are realized through agent harnesses. Therefore, a gap between model assumptions and harness behavior can easily prevent the model's full capabilities from translating into agent performance. We formalize this as the `intent-execution' gap: the mismatch between what the model intends and what the harness executes, and vice versa. We argue that minimizing this intent-execution gap is as important as other aspects of harness design such as tools and execution loops. To illustrate the impact of this harness-model alignment, we develop a simple and customizable harness called `Simple Strands Agent' (SSA). SSA aims to find the bulk of common patterns which generalize across different model families (such as Claude, Gemini, GPT, Grok, Qwen), as well as a small number of model-specific preferences. We make two contributions: (i) we $\textbf{reproduce or improve on the pass@1}$ performance reported by diverse model-provider families on popular agentic benchmarks (SWE-Pro, SWE-Verified and Terminal-Bench-2), and (ii) building on an $\textbf{analysis of 138k trajectories generated by SSA}$, we look beyond the $\texttt{pass@1}$ numbers which tend to be relatively even across frontier models. By representing agent trajectories in code state-spaces, we observe model-level differences in problem-solving behavior. Finer-grained metrics such as edit frequency, testing activity, and phase-transitions reveal how individual models allocate effort across different stages of autonomous problem solving.

### [18] [Can LLMs Be CEOs? Benchmarking Strategic Resource Reallocation with Multi-Role Agent Simulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17459)
*Yuyang Dai,Xueqing Peng,Lingfei Qian,Zhuohan Xie*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: CEO-Bench: A multi-agent benchmark evaluating LLMs on CEO-level strategic resource reallocation by integrating conflicting stakeholder advice under organizational constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing LLM benchmarks focus on isolated cognitive tasks but overlook real executive decision-making challenges: integrating conflicting recommendations from specialized stakeholders under information asymmetry, organizational constraints, and temporal dependencies.

Method: CEO-Bench is a multi-agent benchmark where LLM agents receive conflicting advice from four role-conditioned C-suite advisors (CFO, CTO, COO, CMO) with private signals and distinct priorities. Agents must synthesize these into concrete allocation plans evaluated along four dimensions: role integration, conditional boldness, history-sensitive judgment, and plan validity.

Result: Experiments across five frontier models on 13 scenarios show all models achieve high structural validity but diverge sharply on strategic calibration. Identified systematic failure modes: single-advisor capture, conservative default under ambiguity, historical amnesia. Found structural integration-boldness tradeoff: models engaging more with conflicting perspectives produce less decisive action.

Conclusion: CEO-Bench delineates current LLM capability boundaries as organizational decision-makers, revealing systematic failure modes and integration-boldness tradeoffs, informing future AI-assisted executive system design.

Abstract: Evaluating the decision-making capabilities of large language models (LLMs) is a growing research priority, yet existing benchmarks focus on isolated cognitive tasks such as reasoning, knowledge retrieval, and economic rationality in stylized settings. These evaluations overlook the defining challenge of real executive decision-making: integrating conflicting recommendations from specialized stakeholders under information asymmetry, organizational constraints, and temporal dependencies. We introduce \textsc{CEO-Bench}, a multi-agent benchmark that evaluates LLMs on CEO-level strategic resource reallocation -- the process of redirecting capital across business units in a multi-round, constraint-rich organizational environment. In \textsc{CEO-Bench}, LLM agents receive conflicting advice from four role-conditioned C-suite advisors (CFO, CTO, COO, CMO), each with private signals and distinct priorities, and must synthesize these into a concrete allocation plan evaluated along four dimensions: role integration, conditional boldness, history-sensitive judgment, and plan validity. Experiments across five frontier models on 13 scenarios reveal that all models achieve high structural validity but diverge sharply on strategic calibration -- the hardest capability layer. We identify systematic failure modes including single-advisor capture, conservative default under ambiguity, and historical amnesia, and uncover a structural integration-boldness tradeoff: models that engage more deeply with conflicting perspectives tend to produce less decisive action. These findings delineate the current capability boundary of LLMs as organizational decision-makers and inform the design of future AI-assisted executive systems.

Method: Developed a configurable LLM-as-Judge pipeline that: 1) identifies relevant topics, subtopics, and cognitive demand of questions; 2) assembles verifiable authorized context (syllabus artefacts, prescribed verbs/outcomes, performance descriptors, glossary definitions, marking principles); 3) uses staged LLM workflow to generate question-specific rubrics capturing structured performance expectations; 4) derives and evaluates marking criteria for allocating marks to student responses.

The system has been integrated into an online study platform, with early deployment data providing insights into operational usage and manual overrides.

This paper presents a curriculum-grounded, configurable LLM-as-Judge pipeline for question-level marking, co-developed with an industrial partner, to support exam preparation for university admission. The pipeline identifies the relevant topics, subtopics, and cognitive demand of a question, and assembles verifiable and authorised context to support LLM judgement. Curriculum intent is operationalised through concrete syllabus artefacts, including prescribed verbs and outcomes, performance band descriptors, glossary definitions, and marking-guideline principles. A staged LLM workflow is employed to first generate question-specific rubrics, capturing structured expectations of performance, and then derive and evaluate marking criteria used to allocate marks to student responses. This design improves consistency, transparency, and alignment with official marking practices. Preliminary evaluation shows that the proposed LLM-as-Judge pipeline delivers marking outcomes comparable to human tutors, while yielding justifications that are more traceable to authorised curriculum artefacts and marking standards. The pipeline has also been integrated into an online study platform, where early deployment data provide initial insights into operational usage and manual overrides.

### [20] [SEAGym: An Evaluation Environment for Self-Evolving LLM Agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17546)
*Congjie Zheng,Chuanyi Xue,Bin Liang,Jun Yang,Changshui Zhang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: SEAGym is a new evaluation framework that provides multi-view assessment of LLM agent harness updates, revealing that frequent updates may not improve held-out performance and useful snapshots can degrade over time.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Existing evaluations of self-evolving LLM-based agents are insufficient because they typically reduce the process to isolated task scores or simple sequential curves, making it hard to determine whether updates produce reusable improvements, cause overfitting to recent tasks, increase costs, or harm older behaviors.

Method: SEAGym is introduced as an evaluation environment that measures agent harness updates across training, validation, test, replay, and cost records. It turns Harbor-compatible benchmarks into dynamic self-evolution task sources with train batches, frozen update-validation, held-out ID and OOD transfer views, replay diagnostics, and saved snapshot/metric records. The framework was instantiated on Terminal-Bench 2.0 and HLE benchmarks, comparing ACE, TF-GRPO, and AHE methods under a shared epoch/batch protocol.

Result: Results show that the evaluation views provide complementary signals about evolution: frequent updates may fail to improve held-out performance, useful intermediate snapshots may collapse later, and source diversity plus model backend choice significantly affect harness reliability.

Conclusion: SEAGym provides a comprehensive evaluation framework that reveals important insights about self-evolving agent systems that are obscured by traditional evaluation approaches, highlighting the need for multi-view assessment to understand how agent harness updates truly perform over time.

Abstract: Self-evolving LLM-based agents improve mainly by changing their agent harness: the structured execution layer around a base model, including prompts, memory, tools, middleware, runtime state, and the model-tool interaction loop. Existing evaluations often reduce this process to isolated task scores or a single sequential curve, obscuring whether an update produces reusable improvement, overfits recent tasks, increases cost, or harms older behavior. We introduce SEAGym, an evaluation environment for measuring agent harness updates across training, validation, test, replay, and cost records. SEAGym turns Harbor-compatible benchmarks into dynamic self-evolution task sources with train batches, frozen update-validation, held-out ID and OOD transfer views, replay diagnostics, and saved snapshot and metric records. Instantiating SEAGym on Terminal-Bench 2.0 and HLE, we compare ACE, TF-GRPO, and AHE under a shared epoch/batch protocol. The results show that these evaluation views provide complementary signals about the evolution process: frequent updates may fail to improve held-out performance, useful intermediate snapshots may collapse later, and source diversity and model backend can affect harness reliability.

### [21] [DeepInsight: A Unified Evaluation Infrastructure Across the Physical AI Stack](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17574)
*Siyi Li,Chunyu Sun,Jiahao Zhang,Yuchen Kang,Wuliang Wang,Yu Qiu,Rui Jiang,Haitao Cui,Jie Chen*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: DeepInsight is a unified evaluation infrastructure that spans the full range of Physical AI stack layers with a single runtime and shared traceability, enabling cross-layer regression diagnosis.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current Physical AI evaluation frameworks can't span the full range of operators (varying by three orders of magnitude in scale) - from foundation model decoding to thousands of physics ticks for whole-body control. Existing approaches use separate harnesses that lack shared runtime and scoring, preventing diagnosis of cross-layer regressions.

Method: DeepInsight introduces three narrow abstractions: task, resource, and result, with one invariant per subsystem (one episode driver, one resource-handle protocol for all backends, and one trace identity scheme). It preserves heterogeneity while providing unified infrastructure.

Result: Successfully deployed in production across all three layers of an embodied humanoid stack. Reproduces published references for mature peer frameworks, runs same suites faster on single nodes, scales near-linearly across nodes, and provides unique diagnostic capability through shared trace.

Conclusion: DeepInsight solves the cross-layer evaluation problem by providing unified infrastructure that maintains local validity while enabling cross-layer regression diagnosis - a capability impossible with federated per-segment harnesses.

Abstract: Evaluating a Physical AI stack spans operators that differ by more than three orders of magnitude -- from a single foundation-model decoding step to thousands of physics ticks of whole-body control -- varying orthogonally in modality, reward semantics, and resource profile. No existing framework spans this range, so the stack is evaluated today by stitching together separate harnesses that share neither runtime nor scoring, preserving each segment's local validity but losing the shared identity needed to diagnose cross-layer regressions. We present DeepInsight, an evaluation infrastructure that serves this full spectrum on a single runtime. Rather than homogenize the regimes, it preserves their heterogeneity behind three narrow abstractions -- task, resource, and result -- each realized as one invariant shared by every subsystem: one episode driver, one resource-handle protocol implemented by every expensive backend (LLM inference and sandboxed runtimes alike), and one trace identity scheme under which every event is written. Deployed in production across all three layers of an embodied humanoid stack, this single set of invariants onboards new benchmarks largely by configuration. Where mature peer orchestrators exist -- at the foundation-model end -- it reproduces published references and peer-framework readings within their own spread, runs the same suites faster on a single node, and scales near-linearly across nodes. Its distinctive return is diagnostic: because every layer writes into one shared trace, a regression that begins in one layer and surfaces in another stays localizable on that trace -- a cross-layer payoff no federation of per-segment harnesses can reproduce.

</details>


### [22] [Surrogate Assisted Pedestrian Protection Design via a Foundation Model Orchestrated Workflow](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17577)
*Osamu Ito,Akihiko Katagiri,Yoshikazu Nakagawa,Shin Saeki,Jun Shiraishi,Masato Sasaki*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: First foundation model-orchestrated workflow for crash safety design that reduces evaluation time from hours per simulation to seconds, enabling rapid exploration of pedestrian protection designs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Crash safety design poses unique challenges compared to other engineering domains due to highly nonlinear contact dynamics, material nonlinearity, and discrete state transitions that are difficult to model with data-driven surrogates. Conventional CAE simulations are slow (hours per simulation), limiting design exploration.

Method: Four-component integrated workflow: (1) Surrogate model trained on CAE simulations to predict pedestrian leg injury metrics with conformal prediction intervals, (2) Multiobjective evolutionary search (NSGA-II) for diverse feasible parameter discovery, (3) Morphing-based geometry generator for topology-preserving 3D shapes, (4) Natural-language interface with LLM orchestrating workflow and vision-language model for semantic design comparison.

Result: Achieved average R²=0.87 for injury metric prediction; Generated 35 distinct safety-compliant alternatives in a single exploration for automotive front-bumper case study; Reduced evaluation time from hours per CAE simulation to seconds; Demonstrated workflow that would take weeks with conventional CAE iteration.

Conclusion: Foundation models can effectively serve as integration layers between ML surrogates and physics-based simulation, enabling AI capabilities in safety-critical engineering domains like crash safety design, with significant time savings and design exploration improvements.

Abstract: AI-driven engineering workflows face particular challenges in crash safety design: unlike aerodynamics, crash events involve highly nonlinear contact dynamics, material nonlinearity, and discrete state transitions that are difficult to capture with data-driven surrogate models. To the best of our knowledge, we present the first foundation model--orchestrated workflow for crash safety design that enables surrogate-assisted exploration for pedestrian protection, reducing evaluation time from hours per CAE simulation to seconds.
  The workflow integrates four components: (1) a surrogate trained on CAE crash simulations to predict pedestrian leg injury metrics from design parameters, achieving an average $R^2=0.87$ and providing distribution-free conformal prediction intervals; (2) multiobjective evolutionary search (NSGA-II) to discover diverse feasible parameter sets under user-specified constraints; (3) a morphing-based geometry generator that maps parameters to topology-preserving 3D shapes; and (4) a natural-language interface in which an LLM orchestrates the workflow and a vision--language model supports semantic comparison of generated designs.
  In an automotive front-bumper case study, the workflow produces 35 distinct safety-compliant alternatives from a single exploration, a process that would require weeks with conventional CAE iteration. These results suggest that foundation models can serve as integration layers between ML surrogates and physics-based simulation, helping bring AI capabilities to safety-critical engineering domains.

### [23] [Closing the Feedback Loop: From Experience Extraction to Insight Governance in Verbal Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17591)
*Yanwei Cui,Xing Zhang,Yulong Zhang,Li Shao,Xiaofeng Shi,Guanghui Wang,Peiyang He*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Training-free verbal RL enables LLM agents to learn from world feedback via verbal rules, but faces retention-forgetting dilemma in non-stationary environments. Proposed three-layer architecture with curation loop solves governance gap and dramatically improves performance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM agents using training-free verbal reinforcement learning can learn from objective world feedback (task outcomes, market returns, etc.) without parameter changes. However, in non-stationary environments they face a fundamental retention-forgetting dilemma: keeping stale insights causes negative transfer, while discarding them leads to catastrophic forgetting when conditions recur.

Method: Proposes a three-layer architecture (rules, evidence, and skills) connected by a feedback-driven curation loop. Rules capture distilled experience from world outcomes; evidence logs track each rule's reliability across episodes; skills govern rule application, conflict resolution, and abstention decisions. Addresses four requirements: outcome-driven evaluation, persistent structured evidence, non-monotonic knowledge lifecycle, and compositional governance.

Result: On financial forecasting case study (where world feedback is abundant, noisy, and non-stationary), shows that accumulated experience either degrades performance below zero-shot baseline or dramatically improves accuracy and risk-adjusted returns, depending solely on whether the curation loop is present.

Conclusion: The curation loop is critical for navigating the retention-forgetting dilemma in non-stationary environments. Existing methods over-invest in experience extraction while under-investing in insight governance. The proposed architecture closes this governance gap and enables reliable learning from world feedback.

Abstract: Training-free verbal reinforcement learning enables LLM agents to learn from world feedback -- objective signals such as dynamic task outcomes, market returns, or demand forecasts -- by extracting verbal rules from experience and injecting them as context, updating the agent's behavior without parameter changes. However, in non-stationary environments these agents face a retention-forgetting dilemma: retaining stale insights causes negative transfer, while discarding them causes catastrophic forgetting when conditions recur. We identify four requirements for navigating this dilemma -- outcome-driven evaluation, persistent structured evidence, non-monotonic knowledge lifecycle, and compositional governance -- and show that existing methods invest heavily in experience extraction while underinvesting in insight governance. We propose a three-layer architecture -- rules, evidence, and skills -- connected by a feedback-driven curation loop that closes the governance gap. Rules capture distilled experience from world outcomes; evidence logs track each rule's reliability across episodes; skills govern which rules to apply, how to resolve conflicts, and when to abstain. On financial forecasting as a case study, where world feedback is naturally abundant, noisy, and non-stationary, we show that the same accumulated experience either degrades performance below the zero-shot baseline or dramatically improves accuracy and risk-adjusted returns, depending on whether the curation loop is present.

### [24] [Brick-DICL: Dynamic In-Context Learning for Automated Brick Schema Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.17637)
*Yiyue Qian,Shinan Zhang,Huan Song,Negin Sokhandan,Hannah Marlowe,Diego Socolinsky*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Brick-DICL is a two-stage dynamic in-context learning framework that automates Brick schema classification for building management systems, using RAG techniques and multi-LLM filtering to overcome standardization challenges.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The lack of standardization across BMS points from different manufacturers creates significant barriers to integration and data utilization in building management systems. While the Brick schema offers a standardized ontology, mapping BMS points to appropriate Brick classes presents three critical challenges: (1) extensive number of Brick classes (936 in latest version), (2) limited domain-specific knowledge in LLMs, and (3) substantial manual effort required for verification.

Method: Proposes Brick-DICL, a two-stage dynamic in-context learning framework consisting of: metadata-RAG (retrieves relevant examples to enhance LLMs' domain knowledge) and class-RAG (narrows down potential Brick classes to address large classification space). Implements multi-LLM filtering mechanism that compares predictions across multiple models, flagging low-confidence classifications for human review.

Result: Brick-DICL achieves significant classification accuracy improvements on building datasets, outperforming existing methods. It's applicable to any building management system regardless of manufacturer or metadata format, reduces manual verification effort, and enables rapid digital building onboarding. Extensive experiments demonstrate effectiveness across diverse building datasets.

Conclusion: Brick-DICL accelerates the path toward standardized, interoperable building management systems by providing an automated, efficient, and accurate solution for Brick schema classification that addresses the key challenges of scale, domain knowledge, and manual effort.

Abstract: Building Management Systems (BMS) are essential for optimizing energy efficiency and operational performance in modern buildings. However, the lack of standardization across BMS points from different manufacturers creates significant barriers to integration and data utilization. While the Brick schema offers a standardized ontology for building systems, mapping BMS points to appropriate Brick classes presents three critical challenges: (i) the extensive number of Brick classes (936 in the latest version), (ii) limited domain-specific knowledge in large language models (LLMs), and (iii) substantial manual effort required for verification. To address these challenges, we propose Brick-DICL, a two-stage dynamic in-context learning framework for automated Brick schema classification. Brick-DICL consists of two primary components: metadata-RAG, which retrieves relevant examples to enhance LLMs' domain knowledge, and class-RAG, which narrows down potential Brick classes to address the large classification space. Additionally, we implement a multi-LLM filtering mechanism that compares predictions across multiple models, flagging low-confidence classifications for human review. As a result: (i) General: Brick-DICL is applicable to any building management system regardless of manufacturer or metadata format; (ii) Novel and Powerful: as the first dynamic in-context learning approach for Brick schema classification, Brick-DICL achieves significant classification accuracy improvements on building datasets, outperforming existing methods; (iii) Efficient: our multi-LLM filtering strategy reduces manual verification effort, enabling rapid digital building onboarding. Extensive experiments demonstrate Brick-DICL's effectiveness across diverse building datasets, accelerating the path toward standardized, interoperable building management systems.

Abstract: Large language model (LLM) web agents are usually deployed as tool callers: each turn, the model reads a fresh page observation and emits one structured tool action. Recent systems therefore wrap repeated interaction fragments as web skills: callable tools built from successful trajectories or induced programs, so one call can replace several primitives. However, prior skill libraries are still triggered mainly by instruction similarity or coarse site metadata, which yields low skill reuse on held-out sites and leaves much of the potential step and token reduction on the table.

Each induced skill is stored as a transferable interaction pattern (TIP): the skill paired with a structural sketch of the snapshot at induction time. At test time, SkillMigrator retrieves TIPs by layout similarity and grounds their references on the live page. The rest of the stack is standard: accessibility-snapshot observations with stable references, and fixed tool calling over primitives plus skill invocations. Compared with the state-of-the-art approaches, SkillMigrator reduces the average LLM-action count on successful trajectories by 8-10% across both WebArena and Mind2Web at matched success rate.
